href="/wiki/Federal_Information_Processing_Standards" title="Federal Information Processing Standards">FIPS code</a></th><td>\n42-60000</td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row">Major airport</th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_International_Airport" title="Philadelphia International Airport">Philadelphia International Airport</a></td></tr><tr class="mergedrow"><th scope="row"><a href="/wiki/Interstate_Highway_System" title="Interstate Highway System">Interstates</a></th><td>\n<a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Pennsylvania" title="Interstate 95 in Pennsylvania"><img alt="I-95 (PA).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/I-95_%28PA%29.svg/26px-I-95_%28PA%29.svg.png" width="26" height="26" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/22/I-95_%28PA%29.svg/39px-I-95_%28PA%29.svg.png 1.5x, title="Interstate 76 in Pennsylvania"><img alt="I-76 (PA).svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/I-76_%28PA%29.svg/26px-I-76_%28PA%29.svg.png" width="26" height="26" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/I-76_%28PA%29.svg/39px-I-76_%28PA%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/I-76_%28PA%29.svg/52px-I-76_%28PA%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="601" data-file-height="601" /></a> <a href="/wiki/Interstate_676" title="Interstate 676"><img alt="I-676.svg" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/I-676.svg/26px-I-676.svg.png" width="26" height="21" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/I-676.svg/39px-I-676.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e9/I-676.svg/52px-I-676.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="751" data-file-height="601" /></a></td></tr><tr class="mergedtoprow"><th scope="row">Website</th><td>\n<a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.phila.gov/">www.phila.gov</a></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Philadelphia</b>, often called <b>Philly</b>, is the largest city in the <a href="/wiki/U.S._state" title="U.S. state">U.S. state</a> and <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_(U.S._state)" title="Commonwealth (U.S. state)">Commonwealth</a> of <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania" title="Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/List_of_United_States_cities_by_population" title="List of United States cities by population">sixth-most populous U.S. city</a>, with a 2017 census-estimated population of 1,580,863.<sup id="cite_ref-Pop_Estimate_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pop_Estimate-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Consolidation,_1854" title="Act of Consolidation, 1854">Since 1854</a>, the city has been coterminous with <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia County, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia County</a>, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_counties_in_Pennsylvania" title="List of counties in Pennsylvania">most populous county</a> in Pennsylvania and the urban core of the <a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_statistical_areas" title="List of metropolitan statistical areas">eighth-largest U.S. metropolitan statistical area</a>, with over 6&#160;million residents as of  2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Philadelphia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-2017MSAest_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2017MSAest-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia is also the economic and cultural anchor of the greater <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Valley" title="Delaware Valley">Delaware Valley</a>, located along the lower <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill</a> Rivers, within the <a href="/wiki/Northeast_megalopolis" title="Northeast megalopolis">Northeast megalopolis</a>. The Delaware Valley\'s population of 7.2&#160;million ranks it as the <a href="/wiki/Combined_statistical_area#List_of_combined_statistical_areas" title="Combined statistical area">eighth-largest combined statistical area</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-2017CSAest_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2017CSAest-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a>, an English <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quaker</a>, founded the city in 1682 to serve as capital of the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania Colony</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia played an instrumental role in the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">American Revolution</a> as a meeting place for the <a href="/wiki/Founding_Fathers_of_the_United_States" title="Founding Fathers of the United States">Founding Fathers of the United States</a>, who signed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">Declaration of Independence</a> in 1776 at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Constitution" title="United States Constitution">Constitution</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Philadelphia Convention</a> of 1787. Several other key events occurred in Philadelphia during the <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">Revolutionary War</a> including the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a>, the preservation of the <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Bell#Early_days_(1754\xe2\x80\x931846)" title="Liberty Bell">Liberty Bell</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Germantown" title="Battle of Germantown">Battle of Germantown</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Siege_of_Fort_Mifflin" title="Siege of Fort Mifflin">Siege of Fort Mifflin</a>. Philadelphia was one of the nation\'s <a href="/wiki/List_of_capitals_in_the_United_States" title="List of capitals in the United States">capitals</a> during the revolution, and served as temporary U.S. capital while <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington, D.C.</a>, was under construction. In the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a>, Philadelphia became a major industrial center and a railroad hub. The city grew from an influx of European <a href="/wiki/Immigration" title="Immigration">immigrants</a>, most of whom came from Ireland, Italy and Germany\xe2\x80\x94the three largest reported ancestry groups in the city as of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Philadelphia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>.<sup id="cite_ref-census.gov_ancestry_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-census.gov_ancestry-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> In the early <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th century</a>, Philadelphia became a prime destination for <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a> after the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">Civil War</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> as well as <a href="/wiki/Puerto_Ricans_in_Philadelphia" title="Puerto Ricans in Philadelphia">Puerto Ricans</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-VazquezHernandezp90_11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-VazquezHernandezp90-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> The city\'s population doubled from one million to two million people between 1890 and 1950.\n</p><p>The Philadelphia area\'s many universities and colleges make it a top study destination, as the city has evolved into an educational and economic hub.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-WHC_13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> According to the <a href="/wiki/Bureau_of_Economic_Analysis" title="Bureau of Economic Analysis">Bureau of Economic Analysis</a>, the Philadelphia area had a <a href="/wiki/Gross_domestic_product" title="Gross domestic product">gross domestic product</a> of US$431&#160;billion in 2016, the <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._metropolitan_areas_by_GDP" title="List of U.S. metropolitan areas by GDP">eighth-largest metropolitan economy</a> in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-Gross_Metropolitan_Product_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gross_Metropolitan_Product-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia is the center of economic activity in Pennsylvania and is home to five <a href="/wiki/Fortune_1000" title="Fortune 1000">Fortune 1000</a> companies. The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_skyline" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia skyline">Philadelphia skyline</a> is expanding, with a market of almost 81,900 commercial properties in 2016,<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup> including several nationally prominent <a href="/wiki/Skyscraper" title="Skyscraper">skyscrapers</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Comcast2_16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comcast2-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia has more outdoor <a href="/wiki/Sculpture" title="Sculpture">sculptures</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mural" title="Mural">murals</a> than any other American city.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>, when combined with the adjacent <a href="/wiki/Wissahickon_Valley_Park" title="Wissahickon Valley Park">Wissahickon Valley Park</a> in the same <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watershed</a>, is one of the largest contiguous <a href="/wiki/Urban_park" title="Urban park">urban park</a> areas in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-tplrank_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tplrank-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup> The city is known for its arts, culture, cuisine, and <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">colonial history</a>, attracting 42 million domestic tourists in 2016 who spent US$6.8 billion, generating an estimated $11 billion in total economic impact in the city and surrounding four counties of Pennsylvania.<sup id="cite_ref-Visit_Philadelphia_20-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Visit_Philadelphia-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia has also emerged as a <a href="/wiki/Biotechnology" title="Biotechnology">biotechnology</a> hub.<sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia is the birthplace of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup> and is also the home of many U.S. <a href="/wiki/List_of_Pennsylvania_firsts" title="List of Pennsylvania firsts">firsts</a>, including the first <a href="/wiki/Library_Company_of_Philadelphia" title="Library Company of Philadelphia">library</a> (1731),<sup id="cite_ref-Firsts_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firsts-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Hospital" title="Pennsylvania Hospital">hospital</a> (1751),<sup id="cite_ref-Firsts_24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firsts-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Perelman_School_of_Medicine_at_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Perelman School of Medicine at the University of Pennsylvania">medical school</a> (1765),<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">national capital</a> (1774),<sup id="cite_ref-senate_26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-senate-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Stock_Exchange" title="Philadelphia Stock Exchange">stock exchange</a> (1790),<sup id="cite_ref-Firsts_24-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Firsts-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Zoo" title="Philadelphia Zoo">zoo</a> (1874),<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">&#91;27&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Wharton_School_of_the_University_of_Pennsylvania" title="Wharton School of the University of Pennsylvania">business school</a> (1881).<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">&#91;28&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia contains 67 <a href="/wiki/List_of_National_Historic_Landmarks_in_Philadelphia" title="List of National Historic Landmarks in Philadelphia">National Historic Landmarks</a> and the <a href="/wiki/World_Heritage_Site" class="mw-redirect" title="World Heritage Site">World Heritage Site</a> of <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall" title="Independence Hall">Independence Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-29" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-29">&#91;29&#93;</a></sup> The city became a member of the <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_World_Heritage_Cities" title="Organization of World Heritage Cities">Organization of World Heritage Cities</a> in 2015,<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">&#91;30&#93;</a></sup> as the first World Heritage City in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-WHC_13-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHC-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup> Although Philadelphia is rapidly undergoing <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">gentrification</a>, the city actively maintains mitigation strategies to minimize displacement of homeowners in gentrifying neighborhoods.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">&#91;31&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="toclimit-3"><div id="toc" class="toc"><input type="checkbox" role="button" id="toctogglecheckbox" class="toctogglecheckbox" links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Philadelphia" title="History of Philadelphia">History of Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Timeline_of_Philadelphia" title="Timeline of Philadelphia">Timeline of Philadelphia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:1752_(_1850_)_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_(first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House)_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg/170px-1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg" width="170" height="282" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg/255px-1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fe/1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg/340px-1752_%28_1850_%29_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_%28first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House%29_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2410" data-file-height="4000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:1752_(_1850_)_Scull_%5E_Heap_Map_of_Philadelphia_%5E_Environs_(first_view_of_Phillidelphia_State_House)_-_Geographicus_-_Philadelphia-sculllobach-1850.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 18th-century map of Philadelphia, circa 1752</div></div></div>\n<p>Before Europeans arrived, the Philadelphia area was home to the <a href="/wiki/Lenape" title="Lenape">Lenape (Delaware)</a> <a href="/wiki/Native_Americans_in_the_United_States" title="Native Americans in the United States">Indians</a> in the village of <a href="/wiki/Shackamaxon" title="Shackamaxon">Shackamaxon</a>. The Lenape are a Native American tribe and <a href="/wiki/First_Nations" title="First Nations">First Nations</a> <a href="/wiki/Band_government" title="Band government">band government</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-p422_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-p422-32">&#91;32&#93;</a></sup> They are also called Delaware Indians,<sup id="cite_ref-josephy_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-josephy-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> and their historical territory was along the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">watershed</a>, western <a href="/wiki/Long_Island" title="Long Island">Long Island</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Hudson_Valley" title="Hudson Valley">Lower Hudson Valley</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> Most Lenape were pushed out of their Delaware homeland during the 18th century by expanding European colonies, exacerbated by losses from intertribal conflicts.<sup id="cite_ref-josephy_33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-josephy-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> Lenape communities were weakened by newly introduced diseases, mainly <a href="/wiki/Smallpox" title="Smallpox">smallpox</a>, and violent conflict with Europeans. <a href="/wiki/Iroquois" title="Iroquois">Iroquois</a> people occasionally fought the Lenape. Surviving Lenape moved west into the upper <a href="/wiki/Ohio_River" title="Ohio River">Ohio River</a> basin. The <a href="/wiki/American_Revolutionary_War" title="American Revolutionary War">American Revolutionary War</a> and United States\' independence pushed them further west. In the 1860s, the United States government sent most Lenape remaining in the <a href="/wiki/Eastern_United_States" title="Eastern United States">eastern United States</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Territory" title="Indian Territory">Indian Territory</a> (present-day Oklahoma and surrounding territory) under the <a href="/wiki/Indian_removal" title="Indian removal">Indian removal</a> policy. In the 21st century, most Lenape reside in <a href="/wiki/Oklahoma" title="Oklahoma">Oklahoma</a>, with some communities living also in <a href="/wiki/Wisconsin" title="Wisconsin">Wisconsin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ontario" title="Ontario">Ontario</a> (Canada), and in their traditional homelands.\n</p><p>Europeans came to the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_Valley" title="Delaware Valley">Delaware Valley</a> in the early 17th century, with the first settlements founded by the Dutch, who in 1623 built <a href="/wiki/Fort_Nassau_(South_River)" title="Fort Nassau (South River)">Fort Nassau</a> on the Delaware River opposite the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a> in what is now <a href="/wiki/Brooklawn,_New_Jersey" title="Brooklawn, New Jersey">Brooklawn, New Jersey</a>. The Dutch considered the entire Delaware River valley to be part of their <a href="/wiki/New_Netherland" title="New Netherland">New Netherland</a> colony. In 1638, <a href="/wiki/Sweden" title="Sweden">Swedish</a> settlers led by renegade Dutch established the colony of <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">New Sweden</a> at <a href="/wiki/Fort_Christina" title="Fort Christina">Fort Christina</a> (present-day <a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington, Delaware</a>) and quickly spread out in the valley. In 1644, New Sweden supported the <a href="/wiki/Susquehannock" title="Susquehannock">Susquehannocks</a> in their military defeat of the English colony of <a href="/wiki/Maryland" title="Maryland">Maryland</a>. In 1648, the Dutch built <a href="/wiki/Fort_Beversreede" title="Fort Beversreede">Fort Beversreede</a> on the west bank of the Delaware, south of the Schuylkill near the present-day <a href="/wiki/Eastwick,_Philadelphia" title="Eastwick, Philadelphia">Eastwick</a> neighborhood, to reassert their dominion over the area. The Swedes responded by building <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden#Forts" title="New Sweden">Fort Nya Korsholm</a>, or New <a href="/wiki/Korsholm" title="Korsholm">Korsholm</a>, after a town in <a href="/wiki/Finland" title="Finland">Finland</a> with a Swedish majority. In 1655, a Dutch military campaign led by New Netherland Director-General <a href="/wiki/Peter_Stuyvesant" title="Peter Stuyvesant">Peter Stuyvesant</a> took control of the Swedish colony, ending its claim to independence. The Swedish and Finnish settlers continued to have their own militia, religion, and court, and to enjoy substantial autonomy under the Dutch. The English conquered the New Netherland colony in 1664, though the situation did not change substantially until 1682 when the area was included in <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a>\'s charter for Pennsylvania.\n</p><p>In 1681, in partial repayment of a debt, <a href="/wiki/Charles_II_of_England" title="Charles II of England">Charles II of England</a> granted Penn a <a href="/wiki/Charter" title="Charter">charter</a> for what would become the <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Pennsylvania colony</a>. Despite the royal charter, Penn bought the land from the local Lenape to be on good terms with the Native Americans and ensure peace for his colony.<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> Penn made a treaty of friendship with Lenape chief <a href="/wiki/Tamanend" title="Tamanend">Tammany</a> under an elm tree at <a href="/wiki/Shackamaxon" title="Shackamaxon">Shackamaxon</a>, in what is now the city\'s <a href="/wiki/Fishtown,_Philadelphia" title="Fishtown, Philadelphia">Fishtown</a> neighborhood.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> \nPenn named the city Philadelphia, which is <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> for "brotherly love," derived from the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a> terms <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%CF%86%CE%AF%CE%BB%CE%BF%CF%82" class="extiw" title="wikt:\xcf\x86\xce\xaf\xce\xbb\xce\xbf\xcf\x82">\xcf\x86\xce\xaf\xce\xbb\xce\xbf\xcf\x82</a> <i>ph\xc3\xadlos</i> (beloved, dear) and <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%80%CE%B4%CE%B5%CE%BB%CF%86%CF%8C%CF%82" class="extiw" title="wikt:\xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xcf\x86\xcf\x8c\xcf\x82">\xe1\xbc\x80\xce\xb4\xce\xb5\xce\xbb\xcf\x86\xcf\x8c\xcf\x82</a> <i>adelph\xc3\xb3s</i> (brother, brotherly). As a <a href="/wiki/Quakers" title="Quakers">Quaker</a>, Penn had experienced religious persecution and wanted his colony to be a place where anyone could worship freely. This tolerance, far more than afforded by most other colonies, led to better relations with the local native tribes and fostered Philadelphia\'s rapid growth into America\'s most important city.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg/170px-Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg" width="170" height="210" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg/255px-Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/2f/Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg 2x" data-file-width="288" data-file-height="355" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Benjamin_Franklin_by_Jean-Baptiste_Greuze.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, 1777</div></div></div>\n<p>Penn planned a city on the Delaware River to serve as a port and place for government. Hoping that Philadelphia would become more like an English rural town instead of a city, Penn laid out roads on a <a href="/wiki/Grid_plan" title="Grid plan">grid plan</a> to keep houses and businesses spread far apart, with areas for gardens and <a href="/wiki/Orchard" title="Orchard">orchards</a>. The city\'s inhabitants did not follow Penn\'s plans, however, as they crowded by the Delaware River port, and subdivided and resold their lots.<sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Before Penn left Philadelphia for the last time, he issued the Charter of 1701 establishing it as a city. Though poor at first, the city became an important trading center with tolerable living conditions by the 1750s. <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin" title="Benjamin Franklin">Benjamin Franklin</a>, a leading citizen, helped improve city services and founded new ones, such as fire protection, a library, and one of the American colonies\' first hospitals.\n</p><p>A number of philosophical societies were formed, which were centers of the city\'s intellectual life: the Philadelphia Society for Promoting Agriculture (1785), the Pennsylvania Society for the Encouragement of Manufactures and the Useful Arts (1787), the <a href="/wiki/Academy_of_Natural_Sciences_of_Philadelphia" class="mw-redirect" title="Academy of Natural Sciences of Philadelphia">Academy of Natural Sciences</a> (1812), and the <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Institute" title="Franklin Institute">Franklin Institute</a> (1824).<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> These societies developed and financed new industries, attracting skilled and knowledgeable immigrants from Europe.\n</p><p>Philadelphia\'s importance and central location in the colonies made it a natural center for <a href="/wiki/American_Revolution" title="American Revolution">America\'s revolutionaries</a>. By the 1750s, Philadelphia had surpassed <a href="/wiki/Boston" title="Boston">Boston</a> to become the largest city and busiest port in <a href="/wiki/British_America" title="British America">British America</a>, and second in the British Empire after <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup> The city hosted the <a href="/wiki/First_Continental_Congress" title="First Continental Congress">First Continental Congress</a> (1774) before the Revolutionary War; the <a href="/wiki/Second_Continental_Congress" title="Second Continental Congress">Second Continental Congress</a> (1775\xe2\x80\x9376),<sup id="cite_ref-WDL1_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WDL1-42">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> which signed the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Declaration_of_Independence" title="United States Declaration of Independence">United States Declaration of Independence</a>, during the war; and the <a href="/wiki/Constitutional_Convention_(United_States)" title="Constitutional Convention (United States)">Constitutional Convention</a> (1787) after the war. <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_campaign" title="Philadelphia campaign">Several battles</a> were fought in and near Philadelphia as well.\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/220px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" width="220" height="150" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/330px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg/440px-Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1200" data-file-height="816" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Philadelphia_Presidents_house.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/President%27s_House_(Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania)" class="mw-redirect" title="President&#39;s House (Philadelphia, Pennsylvania)">President\'s House</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the presidential mansion of George Washington and John Adams, 1790\xe2\x80\x931800</div></div></div>\n<p>Philadelphia served as the temporary capital of the United States while the new capital was under construction in the <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">District of Columbia</a> from 1790 to 1800.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup> In 1793, the largest <a href="/wiki/1793_Philadelphia_yellow_fever_epidemic" title="1793 Philadelphia yellow fever epidemic">yellow fever epidemic</a> in U.S. history killed approximately 4,000 to 5,000 people in Philadelphia, or about 10% of the city\'s population.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The state capital was moved to <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster</a> in 1799, then <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a> in 1812, while the federal government was moved to Washington, D.C. in 1800 upon completion of the <a href="/wiki/White_House" title="White House">White House</a> and <a href="/wiki/United_States_Capitol" title="United States Capitol">U.S. Capitol</a> building. The city remained the young nation\'s largest until the late <a href="/wiki/18th_century" title="18th century">18th century</a>, being both a financial and a cultural center for America. In 1816, the city\'s free black community founded the <a href="/wiki/African_Methodist_Episcopal_Church" title="African Methodist Episcopal Church">African Methodist Episcopal Church</a> (AME), the first independent black denomination in the country, and the first black Episcopal Church. The free black community also established many schools for its children, with the help of Quakers. <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> surpassed Philadelphia in population by 1790. Large-scale construction projects for new roads, <a href="/wiki/Canal" title="Canal">canals</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rail_transport" title="Rail transport">railroads</a> made Philadelphia the first major <a href="/wiki/Industrial_Revolution" title="Industrial Revolution">industrial</a> city in the United States.\n</p><p>Throughout the <a href="/wiki/19th_century" title="19th century">19th century</a>, Philadelphia hosted a variety of industries and businesses, the largest being <a href="/wiki/Textile_industry" title="Textile industry">textiles</a>. Major corporations in the 19th and early <a href="/wiki/20th_century" title="20th century">20th</a> centuries included the <a href="/wiki/Baldwin_Locomotive_Works" title="Baldwin Locomotive Works">Baldwin Locomotive Works</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Cramp_%26_Sons" title="William Cramp &amp; Sons">William Cramp &amp; Sons Shipbuilding Company</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania Railroad</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Industry, along with the U.S. Centennial, was celebrated in 1876 with the <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Exposition" title="Centennial Exposition">Centennial Exposition</a>, the first official <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World&#39;s fair">World\'s Fair</a> in the United States.\n</p><p>Immigrants, mostly from <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a> and <a href="/wiki/Germany" title="Germany">Germany</a>, settled in Philadelphia and the surrounding districts. These immigrants were largely responsible for the <a href="/wiki/1835_Philadelphia_general_strike" title="1835 Philadelphia general strike">first general strike in North America</a> in 1835, in which workers in the city won the ten-hour workday. The city was a destination for thousands of Irish immigrants fleeing the <a href="/wiki/Great_Famine_(Ireland)" title="Great Famine (Ireland)">Great Famine</a> in the 1840s; housing for them was developed south of <a href="/wiki/South_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="South Street (Philadelphia)">South Street</a> and later occupied by succeeding immigrants. They established a network of <a href="/wiki/Catholic_church" class="mw-redirect" title="Catholic church">Catholic</a> churches and schools and dominated the Catholic clergy for decades. Anti-Irish, anti-Catholic <a href="/wiki/Nativism_(politics)" title="Nativism (politics)">nativist</a> <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_nativist_riots" title="Philadelphia nativist riots">riots</a> erupted in Philadelphia in 1844. The rise in population of the surrounding districts helped lead to the <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Consolidation,_1854" title="Act of Consolidation, 1854">Act of Consolidation of 1854</a>, which extended the city limits from the 2 square miles (5.2&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a> to the roughly 134 square miles (350&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia County, Pennsylvania">Philadelphia County</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Consolidation_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Consolidation-48">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>\nIn the latter half of the century, immigrants from <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eastern_Europe" title="Eastern Europe">Eastern Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Italy" title="Italy">Italy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/African_Americans" title="African Americans">African Americans</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Southern_United_States" title="Southern United States">southern U.S.</a> settled in the city.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia was represented by the <a href="/wiki/Washington_Grays_(Philadelphia)" title="Washington Grays (Philadelphia)">Washington Grays</a> in the <a href="/wiki/American_Civil_War" title="American Civil War">American Civil War</a>. The African-American population of Philadelphia increased from 31,699 to 219,559 between 1880 and 1930.<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> Twentieth-century black newcomers were part of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Migration_(African_American)" title="Great Migration (African American)">Great Migration</a> out of the rural south to northern and midwestern industrial cities.\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 175.33333333333px"><div style="width: 175.33333333333px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 173.33333333333px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a Indians</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_West" title="Benjamin West">Benjamin West</a>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 183.33333333333px"><div data-file-width="2976" data-file-height="1969" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p><a href="/wiki/Declaration_of_Independence_(Trumbull)" title="Declaration of Independence (Trumbull)">John Trumbull\'s <i>Declaration of Independence</i></a> - the <a href="/wiki/Committee_of_Five" title="Committee of Five">Committee of Five</a> presents their draft in <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall" title="Independence Hall">Independence Hall</a>, June 28, 1776.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n\t\t<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 198.66666666667px"><div style="width: 198.66666666667px">\n\t\t\t<div class="thumb" style="width: 196.66666666667px;"><div style="margin:0px auto;"><a href="/wiki/File:Centennial_Exhibition,_Opening_Day.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg/295px-Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg" width="197" height="120" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg/442px-Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg/589px-Centennial_Exhibition%2C_Opening_Day.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2548" data-file-height="1558" /></a></div></div>\n\t\t\t<div class="gallerytext">\n<p>Opening day ceremonies at the <a href="/wiki/Centennial_Exhibition" class="mw-redirect" title="Centennial Exhibition">Centennial Exhibition</a> at <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Hall_(Philadelphia)" title="Memorial Hall (Philadelphia)">Memorial Hall</a>, 1876 - first <a href="/wiki/World%27s_fair" title="World&#39;s fair">World\'s Fair</a> in the United States\n</p>\n\t\t\t</div>\n\t\t</div></li>\n</ul>\n<p>By the 20th century, Philadelphia had become known as "corrupt and contented", with an entrenched <a href="/wiki/Republican_Party_(United_States)" title="Republican Party (United States)">Republican</a> <a href="/wiki/Political_machine" title="Political machine">political machine</a> and a complacent population.<sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup> The first major reform came in 1917 when outrage over the election-year murder of a police officer led to the shrinking of the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Council" title="Philadelphia City Council">City Council</a> from two houses to just one.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> In July 1919, Philadelphia was one of more than 36 industrial cities nationally to suffer a race riot of ethnic whites against blacks during <a href="/wiki/Red_Summer" title="Red Summer">Red Summer</a>, in post-World War I unrest, as recent immigrants competed with blacks for jobs. In the 1920s, the public flouting of <a href="/wiki/Prohibition_in_the_United_States" title="Prohibition in the United States">Prohibition</a> laws, <a href="/wiki/Organized_crime" title="Organized crime">organized crime</a>, mob violence, and police involvement in illegal activities led to the appointment of <a href="/wiki/Brigadier_general" title="Brigadier general">Brig. Gen.</a> <a href="/wiki/Smedley_Butler" title="Smedley Butler">Smedley Butler</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">U.S. Marine Corps</a> as director of public safety, but political pressure prevented any long-term success in fighting crime and corruption.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1940, <a href="/wiki/Non-Hispanic_whites" title="Non-Hispanic whites">non-Hispanic whites</a> constituted 86.8% of the city\'s population.<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup> The population peaked at more than two million residents in 1950, then began to decline with the restructuring of industry, which led to the loss of many middle-class union jobs. In addition, <a href="/wiki/Suburbanization" title="Suburbanization">suburbanization</a> had enticed many of the more affluent residents to outlying railroad commuting towns and newer housing. The resulting reduction in Philadelphia\'s tax base and the resources of local government caused the city to struggle through a long period of adjustment, with it approaching bankruptcy by the late 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-58" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-58">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup> \n</p><p>Revitalization and <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">gentrification</a> of neighborhoods began in the late 1970s and continues into the 21st century, with much of the development occurring in the <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a> and <a href="/wiki/University_City,_Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="University City, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">University City</a> neighborhoods. After many of the old manufacturers and businesses left Philadelphia or shut down, the city started attracting service businesses and began to market itself more aggressively as a tourist destination. Contemporary glass-and-granite skyscrapers were built in Center City beginning in the 1980s. Historic areas such as <a href="/wiki/Old_City,_Philadelphia" title="Old City, Philadelphia">Old City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society_Hill" title="Society Hill">Society Hill</a> were renovated during the reformist mayoral era of the 1950s through the 1980s, making those areas among the most desirable neighborhoods in Center City. These developments have begun a reversal of the city\'s population decline between 1950 and 2000 during which it lost about one-quarter of its residents.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup> The city eventually began experiencing a growth in its population in 2007, which has continued with gradual yearly increases to the present.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Pop_Estimate_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pop_Estimate-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geography">Geography</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg/170px-Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg" width="170" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg/255px-Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg/340px-Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="1000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Large_Philadelphia_Landsat.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Landsat_7" title="Landsat 7">Landsat</a> simulated-color image of Philadelphia and the Delaware River</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Topography">Topography</span></h3>\n<p>The geographic center of Philadelphia is located approximately at 40\xc2\xb0 0\xe2\x80\xb2 34\xe2\x80\xb3 north latitude and 75\xc2\xb0 8\xe2\x80\xb2 0\xe2\x80\xb3 west longitude. The <a href="/wiki/40th_parallel_north" title="40th parallel north">40th parallel north</a> passes through neighborhoods in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Philadelphia" title="Northeast Philadelphia">Northeast Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Philadelphia" title="North Philadelphia">North Philadelphia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West Philadelphia</a> including <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>. The city encompasses 142.71 square miles (369.62&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), of which 134.18 square miles (347.52&#160;km<sup>2</sup>) is land and 8.53 square miles (22.09&#160;km<sup>2</sup>), or 6%, is water.<sup id="cite_ref-CenGeoGazetteer_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CenGeoGazetteer-62">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup> Natural bodies of water include the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill</a> rivers, the lakes in <a href="/wiki/Franklin_Delano_Roosevelt_Park" title="Franklin Delano Roosevelt Park">Franklin Delano Roosevelt Park</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cobbs_Creek" title="Cobbs Creek">Cobbs</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wissahickon_Creek" title="Wissahickon Creek">Wissahickon</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pennypack_Creek" title="Pennypack Creek">Pennypack</a> creeks. The largest artificial body of water is the East Park Reservoir in Fairmount Park.\n</p><p>The lowest point is sea level, while the highest point is in <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_Hill,_Philadelphia" title="Chestnut Hill, Philadelphia">Chestnut Hill</a>, about 446 feet (136&#160;m) above sea level on Summit Street near the intersection of <a href="/wiki/Germantown,_Philadelphia" title="Germantown, Philadelphia">Germantown</a> Avenue and <a href="/wiki/Bethlehem_Pike" title="Bethlehem Pike">Bethlehem Pike</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63">&#91;62&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">&#91;63&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia is situated on the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_Seaboard_fall_line" title="Atlantic Seaboard fall line">Fall Line</a> that separates the <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_coastal_plain" title="Atlantic coastal plain">Atlantic coastal plain</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Piedmont_(United_States)" title="Piedmont (United States)">Piedmont</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">&#91;64&#93;</a></sup> The rapids on the Schuylkill River at <a href="/wiki/East_Falls,_Philadelphia" title="East Falls, Philadelphia">East Falls</a> were inundated by the completion of the dam at the <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Water_Works" title="Fairmount Water Works">Fairmount Water Works</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-66" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-66">&#91;65&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The city is the seat of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Philadelphia County, Pennsylvania">its own county</a>. The adjacent counties are <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Montgomery County, Pennsylvania">Montgomery</a> to the northwest; <a href="/wiki/Bucks_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Bucks County, Pennsylvania">Bucks</a> to the north and northeast; <a href="/wiki/Burlington_County,_New_Jersey" title="Burlington County, New Jersey">Burlington County, New Jersey</a>, to the east; <a href="/wiki/Camden_County,_New_Jersey" title="Camden County, New Jersey">Camden County, New Jersey</a>, to the southeast; <a href="/wiki/Gloucester_County,_New_Jersey" title="Gloucester County, New Jersey">Gloucester County, New Jersey</a>, to the south; and <a href="/wiki/Delaware_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Delaware County, Pennsylvania">Delaware County</a> to the southwest.\n</p>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cityscape">Cityscape</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tnone" style="margin-left: auto; margin-right:auto; class="mw-headline" id="City_planning">City planning</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Philadelphia_neighborhoods" title="List of Philadelphia neighborhoods">List of Philadelphia neighborhoods</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG/220px-A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG" width="220" height="156" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG/330px-A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e6/A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG/440px-A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="566" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:A_Portraiture_of_the_City_of_Philadelphia.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A Portraiture of the City of Philadelphia, by <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Holme" title="Thomas Holme">Thomas Holme</a> \xe2\x80\x93 the first map of Philadelphia, ca. 1683</div></div></div>\n<p>Philadelphia\'s central city was created in the 17th century following the plan by <a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a>\'s surveyor <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Holme" title="Thomas Holme">Thomas Holme</a>. <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a> is structured with long straight streets running nearly due east-west and north-south, forming a grid pattern between the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill</a> rivers that is aligned with their courses. The original city plan was designed to allow for easy travel and to keep residences separated by open space that would help prevent the spread of fire.<sup id="cite_ref-Philadelphia_Squares_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philadelphia_Squares-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> Penn planned the creation of five public parks in the city which were renamed in 1824<sup id="cite_ref-Philadelphia_Squares_67-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philadelphia_Squares-67">&#91;66&#93;</a></sup> (new names in parenthesis): Centre Square (Penn Square),<sup id="cite_ref-68" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-68">&#91;67&#93;</a></sup> Northeast Square (<a href="/wiki/Franklin_Square_(Philadelphia)" title="Franklin Square (Philadelphia)">Franklin Square</a>), Southeast Square (<a href="/wiki/Washington_Square_(Philadelphia)" title="Washington Square (Philadelphia)">Washington Square</a>), Southwest Square (<a href="/wiki/Rittenhouse_Square" title="Rittenhouse Square">Rittenhouse Square</a>), and Northwest Square (<a href="/wiki/Logan_Circle_(Philadelphia)" title="Logan Circle (Philadelphia)">Logan Circle/Square</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">&#91;68&#93;</a></sup> Center City had an estimated 183,240 residents as of  2015<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Philadelphia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, making it the second-most populated downtown area in the United States, after <a href="/wiki/Midtown_Manhattan" title="Midtown Manhattan">Midtown Manhattan</a> in New York City.<sup id="cite_ref-70" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-70">&#91;69&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia\'s neighborhoods are divided into large sections\xe2\x80\x94<a href="/wiki/North_Philadelphia" title="North Philadelphia">North</a>, <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Philadelphia" title="Northeast Philadelphia">Northeast</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Philadelphia" title="South Philadelphia">South</a>, <a href="/wiki/Southwest_Philadelphia" title="Southwest Philadelphia">Southwest Philadelphia</a>, <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Philadelphia" title="Northwest Philadelphia">Northwest</a>\xe2\x80\x94surrounding Center City, which corresponds closely with the city\'s limits before consolidation in 1854. Each of these large areas contains numerous neighborhoods, some of whose boundaries derive from the boroughs, townships, and other communities that constituted Philadelphia County before their inclusion within the city.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">&#91;70&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/City_Planning_Commission_(Philadelphia)" title="City Planning Commission (Philadelphia)">City Planning Commission</a>, tasked with guiding growth and development of the city, has divided the city into 18 planning districts as part of the <i>Philadelphia2035</i> physical development plan.<sup id="cite_ref-72" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-72">&#91;71&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-73" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-73">&#91;72&#93;</a></sup> Much of the city\'s 1980 zoning code was overhauled from 2007 to 2012 as part of a joint effort between former mayors <a href="/wiki/John_F._Street" title="John F. Street">John F. Street</a> and <a href="/wiki/Michael_Nutter" title="Michael Nutter">Michael Nutter</a>. The zoning changes were intended to rectify incorrect zoning maps in order to facilitate future community development, as the city forecasts an additional 100,000 residents and 40,000 jobs will be added by 2035.\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Housing_Authority" title="Philadelphia Housing Authority">Philadelphia Housing Authority</a> is the largest landlord in Pennsylvania. Established in 1937, it is the nation\'s fourth-largest housing authority, housing about 84,000 people and employing 1,250. In 2013, its budget was $371&#160;million.<sup id="cite_ref-PHA_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHA-74">&#91;73&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Parking_Authority" title="Philadelphia Parking Authority">Philadelphia Parking Authority</a> works to ensure adequate parking for city residents, businesses and visitors.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">&#91;74&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Architecture">Architecture</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Architecture_of_Philadelphia" title="Architecture of Philadelphia">Architecture of Philadelphia</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_Philadelphia" title="List of tallest buildings in Philadelphia">List of tallest buildings in Philadelphia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg/170px-OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg" width="170" height="255" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg/255px-OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg/340px-OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2140" data-file-height="3210" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:OneLiberyPlacePhiladelphia_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Juxtaposition of architectural styles in <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a>, showing <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Place#One_Liberty_Place" title="Liberty Place">One Liberty Place</a> and <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Hall" title="Philadelphia City Hall">City Hall</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Philadelphia\'s architectural history dates back to <a href="/wiki/Colonial_history_of_the_United_States" title="Colonial history of the United States">colonial</a> times and includes a wide range of styles. The earliest structures were constructed with <a href="/wiki/Log_house" title="Log house">logs</a>, but brick structures were common by 1700. During the 18th century, the <a href="/wiki/Cityscape" title="Cityscape">cityscape</a> was dominated by <a href="/wiki/Georgian_architecture" title="Georgian architecture">Georgian architecture</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall" title="Independence Hall">Independence Hall</a> and <a href="/wiki/Christ_Church,_Philadelphia" title="Christ Church, Philadelphia">Christ Church</a>.\n</p><p>In the first decades of the 19th century, <a href="/wiki/Federal_architecture" title="Federal architecture">Federal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_Revival_architecture" title="Greek Revival architecture">Greek Revival architecture</a> were the dominant styles produced by Philadelphia architects such as <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Henry_Latrobe" title="Benjamin Henry Latrobe">Benjamin Latrobe</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Strickland_(architect)" title="William Strickland (architect)">William Strickland</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Haviland" title="John Haviland">John Haviland</a>, <a href="/wiki/John_Notman" title="John Notman">John Notman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Ustick_Walter" title="Thomas Ustick Walter">Thomas Walter</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Sloan_(architect)" title="Samuel Sloan (architect)">Samuel Sloan</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Philadelphia-Page251_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philadelphia-Page251-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Frank_Furness" title="Frank Furness">Frank Furness</a> is considered Philadelphia\'s greatest architect of the second half of the 19th century. His contemporaries included <a href="/wiki/John_McArthur_Jr." title="John McArthur Jr.">John McArthur Jr.</a>, <a href="/wiki/Addison_Hutton" title="Addison Hutton">Addison Hutton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Eyre" title="Wilson Eyre">Wilson Eyre</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Wilson_Brothers_%26_Company" title="Wilson Brothers &amp; Company">Wilson Brothers</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Horace_Trumbauer" title="Horace Trumbauer">Horace Trumbauer</a>. In 1871, construction began on the <a href="/wiki/Second_Empire_architecture" title="Second Empire architecture">Second Empire</a>-style <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Hall" title="Philadelphia City Hall">Philadelphia City Hall</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Historical_Commission" class="mw-redirect" title="Philadelphia Historical Commission">Philadelphia Historical Commission</a> was created in 1955 to preserve the cultural and architectural history of the city. The commission maintains the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Register_of_Historic_Places" title="Philadelphia Register of Historic Places">Philadelphia Register of Historic Places</a>, adding historic buildings, structures, sites, objects and districts as it sees fit.<sup id="cite_ref-PNC_77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PNC-77">&#91;76&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1932, Philadelphia became home to the first modern <a href="/wiki/International_Style_(architecture)" title="International Style (architecture)">International Style</a> skyscraper in the United States, the <a href="/wiki/PSFS_Building" title="PSFS Building">PSFS Building</a>, designed by <a href="/wiki/George_Howe_(architect)" title="George Howe (architect)">George Howe</a> and <a href="/wiki/William_Lescaze" title="William Lescaze">William Lescaze</a>. The 548&#160;ft (167&#160;m) City Hall remained the tallest building in the city until 1987 when <a href="/wiki/Liberty_Place#One_Liberty_Place" title="Liberty Place">One Liberty Place</a> was completed. Numerous glass and granite skyscrapers were built in Center City beginning in the late 1980s. In 2007, the <a href="/wiki/Comcast_Center" title="Comcast Center">Comcast Center</a> surpassed One Liberty Place to become the city\'s tallest building. The <a href="/wiki/Comcast_Technology_Center" title="Comcast Technology Center">Comcast Technology Center</a> was completed in 2018, reaching a height of 1,121&#160;ft (342&#160;m), as the <a href="/wiki/List_of_tallest_buildings_in_the_United_States" title="List of tallest buildings in the United States">tallest building in the United States</a> outside of <a href="/wiki/Manhattan" title="Manhattan">Manhattan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Chicago" title="Chicago">Chicago</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Comcast2_16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Comcast2-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>For much of Philadelphia\'s history, the typical home has been the <a href="/wiki/Terraced_house" title="Terraced house">row house</a>. The row house was introduced to the United States via Philadelphia in the early 19th century and, for a time, row houses built elsewhere in the United States were known as "Philadelphia rows".<sup id="cite_ref-Philadelphia-Page251_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Philadelphia-Page251-76">&#91;75&#93;</a></sup> A variety of row houses are found throughout the city, from Federal-style continuous blocks in <a href="/wiki/Old_City,_Philadelphia" title="Old City, Philadelphia">Old City</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society_Hill" title="Society Hill">Society Hill</a> to Victorian-style homes in <a href="/wiki/North_Philadelphia" title="North Philadelphia">North Philadelphia</a> to twin row houses in <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West Philadelphia</a>. While newer homes have been built recently, much of the housing dates to the 18th, 19th and early 20th centuries, which has created problems such as <a href="/wiki/Urban_decay" title="Urban decay">urban decay</a> and vacant lots. Some neighborhoods, including <a href="/wiki/Northern_Liberties,_Philadelphia" title="Northern Liberties, Philadelphia">Northern Liberties</a> and <a href="/wiki/Society_Hill" title="Society Hill">Society Hill</a>, have been rehabilitated through <a href="/wiki/Gentrification" title="Gentrification">gentrification</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">&#91;77&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">&#91;78&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">\n\t\t<li style="overflow:hidden;background:#e44;-webkit-print-color-adjust:exact;color-adjust:exact;position:absolute;left:.2em;width:1.2em;bottom:7.7844444444444em;height:1.6333333333333em;">&#160;</div>\n<div style="color:red;position:absolute;bottom:9.4177777777778em; left:-.4em;width:1.6em;height:1.5em;text-align:right"><span style="font-size:80%">7</span></div>\n<div style="color:red;position:absolute;bottom:6.2844444444444em; left:-.4em;width:1.6em;height:1.5em;text-align:right"><span style="font-size:80%">\xe2\x88\x921</span></div>\n</div></td>\n</tr>\n<tr><td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: red">Average max. and min. temperatures in \xc2\xb0C</td></tr>\n<tr><td colspan="12" style="padding: 2px; text-align: left; line-height: 1.5em; color: blue">Precipitation totals in mm</td></tr></tbody></table>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification</a>, Philadelphia falls under the northern periphery of the <a href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical_climate" title="Humid subtropical climate">humid subtropical climate</a> zone (K\xc3\xb6ppen <i>Cfa</i>),<sup id="cite_ref-81" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-81">&#91;80&#93;</a></sup> whereas according to the <a href="/wiki/Trewartha_climate_classification" title="Trewartha climate classification">Trewartha climate classification</a>, the city has a <a href="/wiki/Temperate" class="mw-redirect" title="Temperate">temperate</a> <a href="/wiki/Maritime_climate" class="mw-redirect" title="Maritime climate">maritime climate</a> (<i>Do</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">&#91;81&#93;</a></sup> Summers are typically hot and muggy, fall and spring are generally mild, and winter is moderately cold. The <a href="/wiki/Plant" title="Plant">plant</a> life <a href="/wiki/Hardiness_zone#U.S._cities" title="Hardiness zone">hardiness zones</a> are 7a and 7b, representing an average annual extreme minimum temperature between 0&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9218&#160;\xc2\xb0C) and 10&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9212&#160;\xc2\xb0C).<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">&#91;82&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Snowfall is highly variable with some winters having only light snow while others include major snowstorms. The normal seasonal snowfall averages 22.4&#160;in (57&#160;cm), with rare snowfalls in November or April, and rarely any sustained snow cover.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Seasonal snowfall accumulation has ranged from trace amounts in 1972\xe2\x80\x9373 to 78.7 inches (200&#160;cm) in the winter of 2009\xe2\x80\x9310.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> The city\'s <a href="/wiki/North_American_blizzard_of_1996#Philadelphia_and_Pennsylvania" title="North American blizzard of 1996">heaviest single-storm snowfall</a> was 30.7&#160;in (78&#160;cm) which occurred in January 1996.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">&#91;84&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Precipitation is generally spread throughout the year, with eight to eleven wet days per month,<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">&#91;85&#93;</a></sup> at an average annual rate of 41.5 inches (1,050&#160;mm), but historically ranging from 29.31&#160;in (744&#160;mm) in 1922 to 64.33&#160;in (1,634&#160;mm) in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The most rain recorded in one day occurred on July 28, 2013 when 8.02&#160;in (204&#160;mm) fell at <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_International_Airport" title="Philadelphia International Airport">Philadelphia International Airport</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> Philadelphia has a moderately sunny climate with an average of 2,500 <a href="/wiki/Sunshine_duration" title="Sunshine duration">hours of sunshine</a> annually, and a percentage of sunshine ranging from 47% in December to 61% in June, July, and August.<sup id="cite_ref-noaasun-Phily1961_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noaasun-Phily1961-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The January daily average temperature is 33.0&#160;\xc2\xb0F (0.6&#160;\xc2\xb0C),<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA-txt-Phily1981_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-txt-Phily1981-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> though the temperature frequently rises to 50&#160;\xc2\xb0F (10&#160;\xc2\xb0C) during thaws and dips to 10&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9212&#160;\xc2\xb0C) for 2 or 3 nights in a normal winter.<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA-txt-Phily1981_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-txt-Phily1981-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> July averages 78.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F (25.6&#160;\xc2\xb0C),<sup id="cite_ref-NOAA-txt-Phily1981_89-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-txt-Phily1981-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup> although heat waves accompanied by high humidity and <a href="/wiki/Heat_index" title="Heat index">heat indices</a> are frequent, with highs reaching or exceeding 90&#160;\xc2\xb0F (32&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on 27 days of the year. The average window for freezing temperatures is November 6 thru April 2,<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> allowing a growing season of 217 days. Early fall and late winter are generally dry with February having the lowest average precipitation at 2.64 inches (67&#160;mm). The dewpoint in the summer averages between 59.1&#160;\xc2\xb0F (15&#160;\xc2\xb0C) and 64.5&#160;\xc2\xb0F (18&#160;\xc2\xb0C).<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The highest recorded temperature was 106&#160;\xc2\xb0F (41&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on August 7, 1918, but temperatures at or above 100&#160;\xc2\xb0F (38&#160;\xc2\xb0C) are not common.<sup id="cite_ref-philadelphia1_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philadelphia1-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> The lowest officially recorded temperature was \xe2\x88\x9211&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9224&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on February 9, 1934.<sup id="cite_ref-philadelphia1_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-philadelphia1-90">&#91;88&#93;</a></sup> Temperatures at or below 0&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9218&#160;\xc2\xb0C) are rare with the last such occurrence being <a href="/wiki/1994_North_American_cold_wave" title="1994 North American cold wave">January 19, 1994</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> The record low maximum is 5&#160;\xc2\xb0F (\xe2\x88\x9215&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on February 10, 1899, and December 30, 1880, while the record high minimum is 83&#160;\xc2\xb0F (28&#160;\xc2\xb0C) on July 23, 2011, and July 24, 2010.\n</p><p><br />\n</p>\n<table class="wikitable collapsible collapsed" style="width:90%; text-align:center; style="text-align:center;font-size:95%;">Source: NOAA (relative humidity and sun 1961\xe2\x80\x931990) <sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-NOAA-txt-Phily1981_89-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NOAA-txt-Phily1981-89">&#91;87&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-noaasun-Phily1961_88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-noaasun-Phily1961-88">&#91;86&#93;</a></sup>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Air_quality">Air quality</span></h4>\n<p>Philadelphia County received an <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a> grade of F and a 24-hour <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particle pollution</a> rating of D in the <a href="/wiki/American_Lung_Association" title="American Lung Association">American Lung Association</a>\'s 2017 State of the Air report, which analyzed data from 2013\xe2\x80\x9315.<sup id="cite_ref-ala_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala-96">&#91;91&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-ala2_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala2-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> The city was ranked 22nd for ozone, 20th for short-term particle pollution, and 11th for year-round particle pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-ala3_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala3-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup> According to the same report, the city experienced a significant reduction in high ozone days since 2001\xe2\x80\x94from nearly 50 days per year to fewer than 10\xe2\x80\x94along with fewer days of high particle pollution since 2000\xe2\x80\x94from about 19 days per year to about 3\xe2\x80\x94and an approximate 30% reduction in annual levels of particle pollution since 2000.<sup id="cite_ref-ala2_97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala2-97">&#91;92&#93;</a></sup> Five of the ten largest <a href="/wiki/Combined_statistical_area#List_of_combined_statistical_areas" title="Combined statistical area">combined statistical areas</a> (CSAs) were ranked higher for ozone: <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> (1st), <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a> (9th), <a href="/wiki/Houston" title="Houston">Houston</a> (12th), <a href="/wiki/Dallas" title="Dallas">Dallas</a> (13th), and <a href="/wiki/San_Jose,_California" title="San Jose, California">San Jose</a> (18th). Many smaller CSAs were also ranked higher for ozone including <a href="/wiki/Sacramento,_California" title="Sacramento, California">Sacramento</a> (8th), <a href="/wiki/Las_Vegas" title="Las Vegas">Las Vegas</a> (10th), <a href="/wiki/Denver" title="Denver">Denver</a> (11th), <a href="/wiki/El_Paso,_Texas" title="El Paso, Texas">El Paso</a> (16th), and <a href="/wiki/Salt_Lake_City" title="Salt Lake City">Salt Lake City</a> (20th); however, only two of those same ten CSAs\xe2\x80\x94San Jose and Los Angeles\xe2\x80\x94were ranked higher than Philadelphia for both year-round and short-term particle pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-ala3_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala3-98">&#91;93&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Demographics">Demographics</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Demographics_of_Philadelphia" title="Demographics of colspan="3" style="border-top:1px solid black;font-size:85%;text-align:left">Populations for City of Philadelphia, not for Philadelphia County. Population for Philadelphia County was 54,388 (including 42,520 urban) in 1790; 81,009 (including 69,403 urban) in 1800; 111,210 (including 91,874 urban) in 1810; 137,097 (including 112,772 urban) in 1820; 188,797 (including 161,410 urban) in 1830; 258,037 (including 220,423 urban) in 1840; and 408,762 (including 340,045 urban) in 1850. Under <a href="/wiki/Act_of_Consolidation,_1854" title="Act of Consolidation, 1854">Act of Consolidation, 1854</a>, City of Philadelphia absorbed the various districts, boroughs, townships, other suburbs, and remaining rural area in Philadelphia County as the consolidated City and County of Philadelphia.<br />Source: <sup id="cite_ref-Pop_Estimate_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pop_Estimate-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99">&#91;94&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">&#91;95&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-101" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-101">&#91;96&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102">&#91;97&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">&#91;98&#93;</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>According to the 2017 <a href="/wiki/United_States_Census_Bureau" title="United States Census Bureau">United States Census Bureau</a> estimate, there were 1,580,863 people residing in Philadelphia, representing a 3.6% increase from the 2010 census.<sup id="cite_ref-Pop_Estimate_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pop_Estimate-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> After the <a href="/wiki/1950_United_States_Census" title="1950 United States Census">1950 Census</a>, when a record high of 2,071,605 was recorded, the city\'s population began a long decline. The population dropped to a low of 1,488,710 residents in 2006 before beginning to rise again. Between 2006 and 2017, Philadelphia added 92,153 residents. In 2015, the Census Bureau estimated that the racial composition of the city was 41.5% Black (non-Hispanic), 35.8% White (non-Hispanic), 13.4% Hispanic or Latino, 6.8% Asian, 0.2% Native Americans, 0.03% Pacific Islanders, and 2.0% multiracial.<sup id="cite_ref-2015_Pop_Estimate_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2015_Pop_Estimate-104">&#91;99&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<table
