href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Montgomery County, Pennsylvania">Montgomery</a>, in addition to service to <a href="/wiki/Mercer_County,_New_Jersey" title="Mercer County, New Jersey">Mercer County, New Jersey</a> (<a href="/wiki/Trenton,_New_Jersey" title="Trenton, New Jersey">Trenton</a>) and <a href="/wiki/New_Castle_County,_Delaware" title="New Castle County, Delaware">New Castle County, Delaware</a> (<a href="/wiki/Wilmington,_Delaware" title="Wilmington, Delaware">Wilmington</a> and <a href="/wiki/Newark,_Delaware" title="Newark, Delaware">Newark, Delaware</a>).<sup id="cite_ref-299" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-299">&#91;291&#93;</a></sup> The city\'s subway system consists of two routes: the subway section of the <a href="/wiki/Market%E2%80%93Frankford_Line" title="Market\xe2\x80\x93Frankford Line">Market\xe2\x80\x93Frankford Line</a> running east\xe2\x80\x93west under <a href="/wiki/Market_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Market Street (Philadelphia)">Market Street</a> which opened in 1905 to the west and 1908 to the east of City Hall,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECox196716_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECox196716-300">&#91;292&#93;</a></sup> and the <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_Line" title="Broad Street Line">Broad Street Line</a> running north\xe2\x80\x93south beneath <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Broad Street (Philadelphia)">Broad Street</a> which opened in stages from 1928 to 1938.<sup id="cite_ref-301" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-301">&#91;293&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg/220px-MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg/330px-MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c7/MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg/440px-MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="768" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:MarketFrankfordLineTrain.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Market%E2%80%93Frankford_Line" title="Market\xe2\x80\x93Frankford Line">Market\xe2\x80\x93Frankford Line</a> train departing <a href="/wiki/52nd_Street_station_(Market%E2%80%93Frankford_Line)" title="52nd Street station (Market\xe2\x80\x93Frankford Line)">52nd Street</a> station.</div></div></div>\n<p>Beginning in the 1980s, large sections of the <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Regional_Rail" title="SEPTA Regional Rail">SEPTA Regional Rail</a> service to the far suburbs of Philadelphia were discontinued due to a lack of funding for equipment and infrastructure maintenance.<sup id="cite_ref-mitchell_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mitchell-302">&#91;294&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-303" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-303">&#91;295&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-304" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-304">&#91;296&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia\'s <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">30th Street Station</a> is a major railroad station on Amtrak\'s <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> with 4.4 million passengers in 2017 making it the <a href="/wiki/List_of_busiest_Amtrak_stations" title="List of busiest Amtrak stations">third-busiest</a> station in the country after <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Station_(New_York_City)" title="Pennsylvania Station (New York City)">New York City\'s Pennsylvania Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Washington_Union_Station" title="Washington Union Station">Washington\'s Union Station</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-PA17_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA17-305">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup> 30th Street Station offers access to Amtrak,<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306">&#91;298&#93;</a></sup> SEPTA,<sup id="cite_ref-307" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-307">&#91;299&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/NJ_Transit" title="NJ Transit">NJ Transit</a> lines.<sup id="cite_ref-308" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-308">&#91;300&#93;</a></sup> Over 12 million SEPTA and NJ Transit rail commuters use the station each year, and more than 100,000 people on an average weekday.<sup id="cite_ref-PA17_305-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA17-305">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/PATCO_Speedline" title="PATCO Speedline">PATCO Speedline</a> provides rapid transit service to <a href="/wiki/Camden,_New_Jersey" title="Camden, New Jersey">Camden</a>, <a href="/wiki/Collingswood,_New_Jersey" title="Collingswood, New Jersey">Collingswood</a>, <a href="/wiki/Westmont,_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Westmont, New Jersey">Westmont</a>, <a href="/wiki/Haddonfield,_New_Jersey" title="Haddonfield, New Jersey">Haddonfield</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cherry_Hill,_New_Jersey" title="Cherry Hill, New Jersey">Woodcrest (Cherry Hill)</a>, <a href="/wiki/Voorhees,_New_Jersey" class="mw-redirect" title="Voorhees, New Jersey">Ashland (Voorhees)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lindenwold,_New_Jersey" title="Lindenwold, New Jersey">Lindenwold</a>, New Jersey, from stations on Locust Street between 16th and 15th, 13th and 12th, and 10th and 9th Streets, and on Market Street at 8th Street.<sup id="cite_ref-309" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-309">&#91;301&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Airports">Airports</span></h4>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg/220px-Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg" width="220" height="207" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg/330px-Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg/440px-Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1692" data-file-height="1593" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Control_tower_at_Philadelphia_International_Airport_PHL_-_panoramio.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Control tower at <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_International_Airport" title="Philadelphia International Airport">Philadelphia International Airport</a></div></div></div>\n<p>Two airports serve Philadelphia: the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_International_Airport" title="Philadelphia International Airport">Philadelphia International Airport</a> (PHL) is located 7&#160;mi (11&#160;km) south-southwest of <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a> on the boundary with <a href="/wiki/Delaware_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Delaware County, Pennsylvania">Delaware County</a>, providing scheduled domestic and international air service,<sup id="cite_ref-PHL_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHL-310">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Philadelphia_Airport" title="Northeast Philadelphia Airport">Northeast Philadelphia Airport</a> (PNE) is a <a href="/wiki/General_aviation" title="General aviation">general aviation</a> <a href="/wiki/Relief_airport" title="Relief airport">relief airport</a> in <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Philadelphia" title="Northeast Philadelphia">Northeast Philadelphia</a> serving general and corporate aviation.<sup id="cite_ref-311" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-311">&#91;303&#93;</a></sup>  Philadelphia International Airport is among the busiest airports in the world <a href="/wiki/World%27s_busiest_airports_by_traffic_movements" class="mw-redirect" title="World&#39;s busiest airports by traffic movements">measured by traffic movements</a> (i.e., takeoffs and landings).<sup id="cite_ref-ACI_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ACI-312">&#91;304&#93;</a></sup> More than 30 million passengers pass through the airport annually on 25 airlines, including all major domestic carriers. The airport has nearly 500 daily departures to more than 120 destinations worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-PHL_310-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PHL-310">&#91;302&#93;</a></sup> SEPTA\'s <a href="/wiki/Airport_Line_(SEPTA)" title="Airport Line (SEPTA)">Airport Regional Rail Line</a> provides direct service between Center City railroad stations and Philadelphia International Airport.<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313">&#91;305&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Roads">Roads</span></h4>\n<p><a href="/wiki/William_Penn" title="William Penn">William Penn</a> planned Philadelphia with <a href="/wiki/Numbered_street#Philadelphia" title="Numbered street">numbered streets</a> traversing north and south, and streets named for trees, such as <a href="/wiki/Chestnut_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Chestnut Street (Philadelphia)">Chestnut</a>, <a href="/wiki/Walnut_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Walnut Street (Philadelphia)">Walnut</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Arch_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Arch Street (Philadelphia)">Mulberry</a>, traversing east and west. The two main streets were named <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Broad Street (Philadelphia)">Broad Street</a> (the north-south <a href="/wiki/Arterial_road" title="Arterial road">artery</a>, since designated <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_611" title="Pennsylvania Route 611">Pennsylvania Route 611</a>) and High Street (the east-west artery, since renamed <a href="/wiki/Market_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Market Street (Philadelphia)">Market Street</a>) converging at Centre Square which later became the site of <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Hall" title="Philadelphia City Hall">City Hall</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314">&#91;306&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG/220px-I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG/330px-I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/91/I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG/440px-I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3072" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:I-95_SB_approaching_Girard_Avenue-Lehigh_Avenue.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Traffic heading into Philadelphia on <a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Pennsylvania" title="Interstate 95 in Pennsylvania">Interstate 95</a> during the morning rush hour.</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Interstate_95_in_Pennsylvania" title="Interstate 95 in Pennsylvania">Interstate 95</a> (the Delaware Expressway) traverses the southern and eastern edges of the city along the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> as the main north-south <a href="/wiki/Controlled-access_highway" title="Controlled-access highway">controlled-access highway</a>, connecting Philadelphia with <a href="/wiki/Newark,_New_Jersey" title="Newark, New Jersey">Newark, New Jersey</a> and New York City to the north and with <a href="/wiki/Baltimore" title="Baltimore">Baltimore</a> and Washington, D.C. southward. The city is also served by <a href="/wiki/Interstate_76_(Ohio%E2%80%93New_Jersey)" title="Interstate 76 (Ohio\xe2\x80\x93New Jersey)">Interstate 76</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_Expressway" title="Schuylkill Expressway">Schuylkill Expressway</a>), which runs along the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a>, intersecting the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Turnpike" title="Pennsylvania Turnpike">Pennsylvania Turnpike</a> at <a href="/wiki/King_of_Prussia,_Pennsylvania" title="King of Prussia, Pennsylvania">King of Prussia</a> and providing access to <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a> and points west. <a href="/wiki/Interstate_676" title="Interstate 676">Interstate 676</a> (the Vine Street Expressway) links I-95 and I-76 through Center City by running below street level between the eastbound and westbound lanes of <a href="/wiki/Vine_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Vine Street (Philadelphia)">Vine Street</a>. Entrance and exit ramps for the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Bridge" title="Benjamin Franklin Bridge">Benjamin Franklin Bridge</a> are near the eastern end of the expressway, just west of the I-95 interchange.<sup id="cite_ref-osm_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osm-315">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Roosevelt_Boulevard_(Philadelphia)" title="Roosevelt Boulevard (Philadelphia)">Roosevelt Boulevard</a> and Expressway (<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_1_in_Pennsylvania" title="U.S. Route 1 in Pennsylvania">U.S. 1</a>) connect <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Philadelphia" title="Northeast Philadelphia">Northeast Philadelphia</a> with Center City via I-76 through <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Park" title="Fairmount Park">Fairmount Park</a>. Woodhaven Road (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_63" title="Pennsylvania Route 63">Route 63</a>) and Cottman Avenue (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_73" title="Pennsylvania Route 73">Route 73</a>) serve the neighborhoods of Northeast Philadelphia, running between I-95 and the Roosevelt Boulevard. The Fort Washington Expressway (<a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_309" title="Pennsylvania Route 309">Route 309</a>) extends north from the city\'s northern border, serving <a href="/wiki/Montgomery_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Montgomery County, Pennsylvania">Montgomery County</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bucks_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Bucks County, Pennsylvania">Bucks County</a>. <a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_30_in_Pennsylvania" title="U.S. Route 30 in Pennsylvania">U.S. Route 30</a> (<a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_and_Lancaster_Turnpike" title="Philadelphia and Lancaster Turnpike">Lancaster Avenue</a>) extends westward from <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West Philadelphia</a> to <a href="/wiki/Lancaster,_Pennsylvania" title="Lancaster, Pennsylvania">Lancaster</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-osm_315-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osm-315">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg/220px-Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg" width="220" height="134" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg/330px-Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/b9/Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg 2x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="220" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Ben_Franklin_Bridge_at_sunrise_2009-09-02_06-08-46_4w.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Ben_Franklin_Bridge" class="mw-redirect" title="Ben Franklin Bridge">Ben Franklin Bridge</a> at sunrise</div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Interstate_476" title="Interstate 476">Interstate 476</a> (locally referred to as the Blue Route<sup id="cite_ref-316" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-316">&#91;308&#93;</a></sup>) traverses <a href="/wiki/Delaware_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Delaware County, Pennsylvania">Delaware County</a>, bypassing the city to the west and serving the city\'s western suburbs, as well as providing a link to <a href="/wiki/Allentown,_Pennsylvania" title="Allentown, Pennsylvania">Allentown</a> and points north. <a href="/wiki/Interstate_276" class="mw-redirect" title="Interstate 276">Interstate 276</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Turnpike" title="Pennsylvania Turnpike">Pennsylvania Turnpike</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Turnpike#Delaware_River_extension" title="Pennsylvania Turnpike">Delaware River extension</a>) acts as a bypass and commuter route to the north of the city as well as a link to the <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Turnpike" title="New Jersey Turnpike">New Jersey Turnpike</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York City</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-osm_315-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-osm-315">&#91;307&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River_Port_Authority" title="Delaware River Port Authority">Delaware River Port Authority</a> operates four bridges in the Philadelphia area across the Delaware River to <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>: the <a href="/wiki/Walt_Whitman_Bridge" title="Walt Whitman Bridge">Walt Whitman Bridge</a> (I-76), the <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Bridge" title="Benjamin Franklin Bridge">Benjamin Franklin Bridge</a> (I-676 and U.S. 30), the <a href="/wiki/Betsy_Ross_Bridge" title="Betsy Ross Bridge">Betsy Ross Bridge</a> (<a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Route_90" title="New Jersey Route 90">New Jersey Route 90</a>), and the <a href="/wiki/Commodore_Barry_Bridge" title="Commodore Barry Bridge">Commodore Barry Bridge</a> (<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_322" title="U.S. Route 322">U.S. 322</a> in Delaware County, south of the city).<sup id="cite_ref-317" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-317">&#91;309&#93;</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Burlington_County_Bridge_Commission" title="Burlington County Bridge Commission">Burlington County Bridge Commission</a> maintains two bridges across the Delaware River: the <a href="/wiki/Tacony%E2%80%93Palmyra_Bridge" title="Tacony\xe2\x80\x93Palmyra Bridge">Tacony\xe2\x80\x93Palmyra Bridge</a> which connects <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_73" title="Pennsylvania Route 73">PA Route 73</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Tacony,_Philadelphia,_Pennsylvania" class="mw-redirect" title="Tacony, Philadelphia, Pennsylvania">Tacony</a> section of Northeast Philadelphia with <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Route_73" title="New Jersey Route 73">New Jersey Route 73</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palmyra,_New_Jersey" title="Palmyra, New Jersey">Palmyra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Camden_County,_New_Jersey" title="Camden County, New Jersey">Camden County</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Burlington%E2%80%93Bristol_Bridge" title="Burlington\xe2\x80\x93Bristol Bridge">Burlington\xe2\x80\x93Bristol Bridge</a> which connects <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey_Route_413" title="New Jersey Route 413">NJ Route 413</a>/<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_130" title="U.S. Route 130">U.S. Route 130</a> in <a href="/wiki/Burlington,_New_Jersey" title="Burlington, New Jersey">Burlington, New Jersey</a> with <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Route_413" title="Pennsylvania Route 413">PA Route 413</a>/<a href="/wiki/U.S._Route_13_in_Pennsylvania" title="U.S. Route 13 in Pennsylvania">U.S. 13</a> in <a href="/wiki/Bristol_Township,_Bucks_County,_Pennsylvania" title="Bristol Township, Bucks County, Pennsylvania">Bristol Township</a>, north of Philadelphia.<sup id="cite_ref-318" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-318">&#91;310&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Bus_service">Bus service</span></h4>\n<p>Philadelphia is a hub for <a href="/wiki/Greyhound_Lines" title="Greyhound Lines">Greyhound Lines</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Greyhound_Terminal" title="Philadelphia Greyhound Terminal">Greyhound terminal</a> is located at 1001 Filbert Street (at 10th Street) in Center City, southeast of the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Convention_Center" title="Pennsylvania Convention Center">Pennsylvania Convention Center</a> and south of <a href="/wiki/Chinatown,_Philadelphia" title="Chinatown, Philadelphia">Chinatown</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-319" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-319">&#91;311&#93;</a></sup> Several other bus operators provide service at the Greyhound terminal including <a href="/wiki/Bieber_Transportation_Group" title="Bieber Transportation Group">Bieber Transportation Group</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-320" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-320">&#91;312&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Trailways_Transportation_System" title="Trailways Transportation System">Fullington Trailways</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-321" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-321">&#91;313&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Martz_Group" title="Martz Group">Martz Trailways</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322">&#91;314&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Peter_Pan_Bus_Lines" title="Peter Pan Bus Lines">Peter Pan Bus Lines</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323">&#91;315&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/NJ_Transit_Bus_Operations" title="NJ Transit Bus Operations">New Jersey Transit buses</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-324" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-324">&#91;316&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Other intercity bus services include <a href="/wiki/Megabus_(North_America)" title="Megabus (North America)">Megabus</a> with stops at <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">30th Street Station</a> and the visitor center for <a href="/wiki/Independence_Hall" title="Independence Hall">Independence Hall</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-325" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-325">&#91;317&#93;</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/BoltBus" title="BoltBus">BoltBus</a> (operated by Greyhound) at 30th Street Station.<sup id="cite_ref-326" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-326">&#91;318&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Rail">Rail</span></h4>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_rail_transport_in_Philadelphia" title="History of rail transport in Philadelphia">History of rail transport in Philadelphia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg/220px-Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg" width="220" height="190" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg/330px-Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg/440px-Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="885" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Suburban_Station_Facade.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Suburban_Station" title="Suburban Station">Suburban Station</a> with <a href="/wiki/Art_Deco" title="Art Deco">Art Deco</a> architecture</div></div></div>\n<p>Since the early days of <a href="/wiki/Rail_transportation_in_the_United_States" title="Rail transportation in the United States">rail transportation in the United States</a>, Philadelphia has served as a hub for several major rail companies, particularly the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Railroad" title="Pennsylvania Railroad">Pennsylvania Railroad</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Reading_Company" title="Reading Company">Reading Railroad</a>. The Pennsylvania Railroad first operated <a href="/wiki/Broad_Street_Station_(Philadelphia)" title="Broad Street Station (Philadelphia)">Broad Street Station</a>, then <a href="/wiki/30th_Street_Station" title="30th Street Station">30th Street Station</a> and <a href="/wiki/Suburban_Station" title="Suburban Station">Suburban Station</a>, and the Reading Railroad operated <a href="/wiki/Reading_Terminal" title="Reading Terminal">Reading Terminal</a>, now part of the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Convention_Center" title="Pennsylvania Convention Center">Pennsylvania Convention Center</a>. The two companies also operated competing commuter rail systems in the area. The two systems now operate as a single system under the control of <a href="/wiki/SEPTA" title="SEPTA">SEPTA</a>, the regional transit authority. Additionally, the <a href="/wiki/PATCO_Speedline" title="PATCO Speedline">PATCO Speedline</a> subway system and <a href="/wiki/NJ_Transit" title="NJ Transit">NJ Transit</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_City_Line" title="Atlantic City Line">Atlantic City Line</a> operate successor services to southern <a href="/wiki/New_Jersey" title="New Jersey">New Jersey</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-327" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-327">&#91;319&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>In 1911, Philadelphia had nearly 4,000 electric <a href="/wiki/Tram" title="Tram">trolleys</a> running on 86 lines.<sup id="cite_ref-328" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-328">&#91;320&#93;</a></sup> In 2005, SEPTA reintroduced trolley service to the <a href="/wiki/SEPTA_Route_15" title="SEPTA Route 15">Girard Avenue Line</a>, Route 15.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329">&#91;321&#93;</a></sup> SEPTA operates six "subway-surface" trolleys that run on street-level tracks in <a href="/wiki/West_Philadelphia" title="West Philadelphia">West Philadelphia</a> and subway tunnels in <a href="/wiki/Center_City,_Philadelphia" title="Center City, Philadelphia">Center City</a>, along with two surface trolleys in adjacent suburbs.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330">&#91;322&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Philadelphia is a regional hub of the <a href="/wiki/State-owned_enterprise" title="State-owned enterprise">federally owned</a> <a href="/wiki/Amtrak" title="Amtrak">Amtrak</a> system, with 30th Street Station being a primary stop on the Washington-Boston <a href="/wiki/Northeast_Corridor" title="Northeast Corridor">Northeast Corridor</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Keystone_Corridor" title="Keystone Corridor">Keystone Corridor</a> to <a href="/wiki/Harrisburg,_Pennsylvania" title="Harrisburg, Pennsylvania">Harrisburg</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pittsburgh" title="Pittsburgh">Pittsburgh</a>. 30th Street also serves as a major station for services via the Pennsylvania Railroad\'s former <a href="/wiki/Main_Line_(Pennsylvania_Railroad)" title="Main Line (Pennsylvania Railroad)">Pennsylvania Main Line</a> to Chicago. As of  2017<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Philadelphia&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, 30th Street is Amtrak\'s third-busiest station in the country, after New York City and Washington.<sup id="cite_ref-PA17_305-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PA17-305">&#91;297&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Walk_Score_ranks">Walk Score ranks</span></h4>\n<p>A 2017 study by <a href="/wiki/Walk_Score" title="Walk Score">Walk Score</a> ranked Philadelphia the fifth most walkable major city in the United States with a score of 79 out of 100, in the middle of the "very walkable" range. The city was just edged out by fourth place Miami (79.2), with the top three cities being New York, San Francisco, and Boston. Philadelphia placed fifth in the public transit friendly category, behind Washington, D.C., with the same three cities for walkability topping this category. The city ranked tenth in the bike friendly cities category, with the top three cities being Minneapolis, San Francisco and Portland.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331">&#91;323&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The readers of <a href="/wiki/USA_Today" title="USA Today">USA Today</a> newspaper voted the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River_Trail" title="Schuylkill River Trail">Schuylkill River Trail</a> the best urban trail in the nation in 2015.<sup id="cite_ref-332" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-332">&#91;324&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Utilities">Utilities</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg/220px-Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg" width="220" height="154" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg/330px-Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg/440px-Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4637" data-file-height="3246" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Fairmount_Water_Works_LOC_314409cu.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Water_Works" title="Fairmount Water Works">Fairmount Water Works</a>, Philadelphia\'s second municipal waterworks</div></div></div>\n<p>In 1815, Philadelphia began sourcing its water via the <a href="/wiki/Fairmount_Water_Works" title="Fairmount Water Works">Fairmount Water Works</a> located on the <a href="/wiki/Schuylkill_River" title="Schuylkill River">Schuylkill River</a>, the nation\'s first major urban water supply system. In 1909, the Water Works was decommissioned as the city transitioned to modern <a href="/wiki/Sand_filter" title="Sand filter">sand filtration</a> methods.<sup id="cite_ref-333" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-333">&#91;325&#93;</a></sup> Today, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Water_Department" title="Philadelphia Water Department">Philadelphia Water Department</a> (PWD) provides <a href="/wiki/Drinking_water" title="Drinking water">drinking water</a>, <a href="/wiki/Wastewater" title="Wastewater">wastewater</a> collection, and <a href="/wiki/Stormwater" title="Stormwater">stormwater</a> services for Philadelphia, as well as surrounding counties. PWD draws about 57 percent of its drinking water from the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a> and the balance from the Schuylkill River.<sup id="cite_ref-PWD_334-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWD-334">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup> The city has two filtration plants on the Schuylkill River and one on the Delaware River. The three plants can treat up to 546 million gallons of water per day, while the total storage capacity of the combined plant and distribution system exceeds one billion gallons. The wastewater system consists of three water pollution control plants, 21 pumping stations, and about 3,657 miles (5,885&#160;km) of sewers.<sup id="cite_ref-PWD_334-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PWD-334">&#91;326&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Exelon" title="Exelon">Exelon</a> subsidiary <a href="/wiki/PECO_Energy_Company" title="PECO Energy Company">PECO Energy Company</a>, founded as the Brush Electric Light Company of Philadelphia in 1881 and renamed Philadelphia Electric Company (PECO) in 1902, provides electricity to about 1.6 million customers and more than 500,000 <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> customers in the southeastern Pennsylvania area including the city of Philadelphia and most of its suburbs.<sup id="cite_ref-335" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-335">&#91;327&#93;</a></sup> PECO is the largest electric and natural gas utility in the state with 472 power substations and nearly 23,000 miles (37,000&#160;km) of electric transmission and distribution lines, along with 12,000 miles (19,000&#160;km) of natural gas transmission, distribution &amp; service lines.<sup id="cite_ref-336" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-336">&#91;328&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_Gas_Works" title="Philadelphia Gas Works">Philadelphia Gas Works</a> (PGW), overseen by the <a href="/wiki/Pennsylvania_Public_Utility_Commission" title="Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission">Pennsylvania Public Utility Commission</a>, is the nation\'s largest municipally-owned natural gas utility. PGW serves over 500,000 homes and businesses in the Philadelphia area.<sup id="cite_ref-337" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-337">&#91;329&#93;</a></sup> Founded in 1836, the company came under city ownership in 1987 and has been providing the majority of gas distributed within city limits. In 2014, the <a href="/wiki/Philadelphia_City_Council" title="Philadelphia City Council">City Council</a> refused to conduct hearings on a $1.86 billion sale of PGW, part of a two-year effort that was proposed by the mayor. The refusal led to the prospective buyer terminating its offer.<sup id="cite_ref-338" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-338">&#91;330&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-339" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-339">&#91;331&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>Southeastern Pennsylvania was assigned the <a href="/wiki/Area_code_215" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code 215">215</a> <a href="/wiki/Area_code" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code">area code</a> in 1947 when the <a href="/wiki/North_American_Numbering_Plan" title="North American Numbering Plan">North American Numbering Plan</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Bell_System" title="Bell System">Bell System</a> went into effect. The geographic area covered by the code was split nearly in half in 1994 when <a href="/wiki/Area_code_610" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code 610">area code 610</a> was created, with the city and its northern suburbs retaining 215. <a href="/wiki/Area_code_overlay" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code overlay">Overlay area code</a> 267 was added to the 215 service area in 1997, and 484 was added to the 610 area in 1999. A plan in 2001 to introduce a third overlay code to both service areas (<a href="/wiki/Area_code_445" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code 445">area code 445</a> to 215, <a href="/wiki/Area_code_835" class="mw-redirect" title="Area code 835">area code 835</a> to 610) was delayed and later rescinded.<sup id="cite_ref-340" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-340">&#91;332&#93;</a></sup> Area code 445 was implemented as an overlay for area codes 215 and 267 starting on February 3, 2018.<sup id="cite_ref-341" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-341">&#91;333&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p><br />\nIn 2005, a low-cost, citywide <a href="/wiki/Wi-Fi" title="Wi-Fi">Wi-Fi</a> service was approved for installation in the city. Wireless Philadelphia would have been the first municipal internet utility in a large U.S. city, but the plan was abandoned in 2008 as <a href="/wiki/EarthLink" title="EarthLink">EarthLink</a> pushed back the completion date several times. Mayor Nutter\'s administration closed the project in 2009 after an attempt to revitalize it failed.<sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342">&#91;334&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notable_people">Notable people</span></h2>\n<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_people_from_Philadelphia" title="List of people from Philadelphia">List of people from Philadelphia</a></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Sister_Cities">Sister src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/23px-Flag_of_France.svg.png" width="23" height="15" class="thumbborder" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/35px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/c/c3/Flag_of_France.svg/45px-Flag_of_France.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="900" data-file-height="600" />&#160;</span>France\n</td>\n<td>1999\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Philadelphia has dedicated landmarks to its sister cities. The Sister Cities Park, a site of 0.5 acres (2,400&#160;sq&#160;yd) located at 18th and <a href="/wiki/Benjamin_Franklin_Parkway" title="Benjamin Franklin Parkway">Benjamin Franklin Parkway</a> within <a href="/wiki/Logan_Circle_(Philadelphia)" title="Logan Circle (Philadelphia)">Logan Square</a>, was dedicated in June 1976. The park was built to commemorate Philadelphia\'s first two sister city relationships, with <a href="/wiki/Tel_Aviv" title="Tel Aviv">Tel Aviv</a> and <a href="/wiki/Florence" title="Florence">Florence</a>. The Toru\xc5\x84 Triangle, honoring the sister city relationship with <a href="/wiki/Toru%C5%84" title="Toru\xc5\x84">Toru\xc5\x84</a>, Poland, was constructed in 1976, west of the <a href="/wiki/United_Way_of_America" title="United Way of America">United Way</a> building at 18th Street and the Benjamin Franklin Parkway. Sister Cities Park was redesigned and reopened in 2012, featuring an interactive fountain honoring Philadelphia\'s sister and partnership cities, a caf\xc3\xa9 and visitor\'s center, children\'s play area, outdoor garden, and boat pond, as well as a pavilion built to <a href="/wiki/Environmentally_friendly" title="Environmentally friendly">environmentally friendly</a> standards.<sup id="cite_ref-renovation_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-renovation-355">&#91;347&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356">&#91;348&#93;</a></sup>\n</p><p>The Chinatown Gate, erected in 1984 and crafted by artisans of <a href="/wiki/Tianjin" title="Tianjin">Tianjin</a>, stands astride 10th Street, on the north side of its intersection with <a href="/wiki/Arch_Street_(Philadelphia)" title="Arch Street (Philadelphia)">Arch Street</a>, as a symbol of the sister city relationship. The CDI of Philadelphia has participated in the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Department_of_State" title="United States Department of State">U.S. Department of State</a>\'s "Partners for Peace" project with <a href="/wiki/Mosul" title="Mosul">Mosul</a>, Iraq,<sup id="cite_ref-mosul_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mosul-357">&#91;349&#93;</a></sup> as well as accepting visiting delegations from dozens of other countries.<sup id="cite_ref-ivc_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ivc-358">&#91;350&#93;</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" style="display:table-cell;padding:0.2em 0.2em 0.2em 0.3em;vertical-align:middle"><a href="/wiki/Portal:Philadelphia" title="Portal:Philadelphia">Philadelphia portal</a></span></li></ul></div>\n<ul><li><a href="/wiki/List_of_Metropolitan_Statistical_Areas" class="mw-redirect" title="List of Metropolitan Statistical Areas">Metropolitan areas in the United States</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_metropolitan_areas_in_the_Americas" title="List of metropolitan areas in the Americas">Metropolitan areas in the Americas</a></li>\n<li><a href="/wiki/National_Register_of_Historic_Places_listings_in_Philadelphia" title="National Register of Historic Places listings in Philadelphia">National Register of Historic Places listings in Philadelphia</a></li></ul>\n<div style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">\n<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Description of the Lenape peoples (Delaware nations) historic territories inside the <a href="/wiki/Divides" class="mw-redirect" title="Divides">divides</a> of the frequently mountainous <a href="/wiki/Landforms" class="mw-redirect" title="Landforms">landforms</a> flanking the <a href="/wiki/Delaware_River" title="Delaware River">Delaware River</a>\'s <a href="/wiki/Drainage_basin" title="Drainage basin">drainage basin</a>. These terrains encompass from South to North and then counter-clockwise:\n<div class="hlist hlist-separated hwrap"><ul><li>the shores from the east-shore mouth of the river and the sea coast to Western Long Island (all of both colonial <a href="/wiki/New_Amsterdam" title="New Amsterdam">New Amsterdam</a> and <a href="/wiki/New_Sweden" title="New Sweden">New Sweden</a>), and</li><li>portions of Western Connecticut up to the latitude of the Massachusetts corner of today\'s boundaries\xe2\x80\x94making the eastern bounds of their influence, thence their region extended:</li><li>westerly past the region around <a href="/wiki/Albany,_New_York" title="Albany, New York">Albany, New York</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Susquehanna_River" title="Susquehanna River">Susquehanna River</a> side of the <a href="/wiki/Catskills" class="mw-redirect" title="Catskills">Catskills</a>, then</li><li>southerly through the eastern <a href="/wiki/Pocono_Mountains" title="Pocono Mountains">Poconos</a> outside the rival <a href="/wiki/Susquehannock" title="Susquehannock">Susquehannock</a> lands past <a href="/wiki/Province_of_Pennsylvania" title="Province of Pennsylvania">Eastern Pennsylvania</a> then southerly past the site of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Philadelphia" title="History of Philadelphia">Colonial Philadelphia</a> past the west bank mouth of the Delaware and extending south from that point along a stretch of sea coast in northern colonial <a href="/wiki/Delaware" title="Delaware">Delaware</a>.</li></ul></div>\n<p>The Susquehanna-Delaware watershed divides bound the frequently contested<i> \'hunting grounds\'</i> between the rival <a href="/wiki/Susquehannock" title="Susquehannock">Susquehannock</a> peoples and the Lenape peoples, whilst the Catskills and Berkshires played a similar boundary role in the northern regions of their original colonial era range.\n</p>\n</span></li>\n<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">See <a href="/wiki/North_American_blizzard_of_2009#Snowfall" class="mw-redirect" title="North American blizzard of 2009">North American blizzard of 2009#Snowfall</a> (December 19\xe2\x80\x9320, 2009), <a href="/wiki/February_5%E2%80%936,_2010_North_American_blizzard#Snowfall" title="February 5\xe2\x80\x936, 2010 North American blizzard">February 5\xe2\x80\x936, 2010 North American blizzard#Snowfall</a> (February 5\xe2\x80\x936, 2010), and <a href="/wiki/February_9%E2%80%9310,_2010_North_American_blizzard#Impact" title="February 9\xe2\x80\x9310, 2010 North American blizzard">February 9\xe2\x80\x9310, 2010 North American blizzard#Impact</a> (February 9\xe2\x80\x9310, 2010). The February 2010 storms contributed to a single month record accumulation of 51.5&#160;in (131&#160;cm). If no snow fell outside of February that season, 2009\xe2\x80\x9310 would still rank as 5th-snowiest. See the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20000818012042/http://www.fi.edu/weather/data2/images/wthrsnow.jpg">Franklin Institute</a> for a visual representation of seasonal snowfall.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">The last occurrence of such a temperature was July 18, 2012.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-92"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-92">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Mean monthly maxima and minima (i.e. the highest and lowest temperature readings during an entire month or year) calculated based on data at said location from 1981 to 2010.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-95"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-95">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Official temperature and precipitation measurements for Philadelphia were taken at the Weather Bureau Office in downtown from January 1872 to 19 June 1940, and at Philadelphia Int\'l from 20 June 1940 to the present.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">&#91;89&#93;</a></sup>  Snowfall and snow depth records date to 1 January 1884 and 1 October 1948, respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-PhillyNOAA_84-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-PhillyNOAA-84">&#91;83&#93;</a></sup> In 2006, snowfall measurements were moved to <a href="/wiki/National_Park,_New_Jersey" title="National Park, New Jersey">National Park, New Jersey</a> directly across the Delaware River from the airport.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">&#91;90&#93;</a></sup> </span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-fifteen-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-fifteen_107-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-fifteen_107-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-fifteen_107-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text">From 15% sample</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-130">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">E.g., in the opening chapter of <i>The Handbook of Language Variation and Change</i> (ed. Chambers et al., Blackwell 2002), J.K. Chambers writes that "variationist sociolinguistics had its effective beginnings only in 1963, the year in which William Labov presented the first sociolinguistic research report"; the dedication page of the <i>Handbook</i> says that Labov\'s "ideas imbue every page".</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-245"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-245">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">1,102,620 / 1,567,872 = 70.3% (registered voters divided by 2016 population estimate)</span>\n</li>\n</ol></div></div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>\n<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: decimal;">\n<ol class="references">\n<li id="cite_note-1"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-1">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.librarycompany.org/artifacts/athens.htm">"Art &amp; Artifacts: Discover the Library Company\'s Art and Artifact Collection \xe2\x80\x93 Athens of America"</a>. <i>librarycompany.org</i>.
